<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglican_Communion">Anglican Communion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Anglican Communion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1980, despite their opposing the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a>, More and Fisher were jointly added as martyrs of the reformation to the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>'s calendar of "Saints and Heroes of the Christian Church", to be commemorated every 6 July (the date of More's execution) as "Thomas More, Scholar, and John Fisher, Bishop of Rochester, Reformation Martyrs, 1535".<sup id="cite_ref-CofEholyDays_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CofEholyDays-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>More is also listed in the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Anglican_Church_calendars#Calendars_of_saints&#39;_days_in_churches_throughout_the_Anglican_communion" title="List of Anglican Church calendars">calendars of saints of some of the other churches</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a> including:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Anglican_Church_of_Australia)#July" title="Calendar of saints (Anglican Church of Australia)">The Anglican Church of Australia</a> has "July 6: John Fisher and Thomas More, martyrs (died 1535)".</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Episcopal_Anglican_Church_of_Brazil)" title="Calendar of saints (Episcopal Anglican Church of Brazil)">The Anglican Church of Brazil</a> has "July 6: Thomas More, Martyr, 1535".</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Anglican_Church_of_Canada)#July" title="Calendar of saints (Anglican Church of Canada)">The Anglican Church of Canada</a> has "July 6: Thomas More died 1535 Commemoration" in its <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Anglican_Church_of_Canada)#Book_of_Alternative_Services_Calendar" title="Calendar of saints (Anglican Church of Canada)">Book of Alternative Services Calendar</a>, and has "July 6: The Octave Day of St Peter and St Paul, and Thomas More, Chancellor of England, Martyr 1535." in the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Anglican_Church_of_Canada)#July_2" title="Calendar of saints (Anglican Church of Canada)">July section</a> of its <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Anglican_Church_of_Canada)#Book_of_Common_Prayer_Calendar" title="Calendar of saints (Anglican Church of Canada)">Book of Common Prayer Calendar</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Anglican_Church_of_Southern_Africa)#July" title="Calendar of saints (Anglican Church of Southern Africa)">The Anglican Church of Southern Africa</a> has "July 6: Thomas More, Martyr, 1535".</li></ul>
<p>Among those on which More is not listed are the calendars of the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Episcopal_Church)" title="Calendar of saints (Episcopal Church)">Episcopal Church</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Scottish_Episcopal_Church)" title="Calendar of saints (Scottish Episcopal Church)">Scottish Episcopal Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Hong_Kong_Sheng_Kung_Hui)" title="Calendar of saints (Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui)">Anglican Church in Hong Kong and Macau</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:APSjfb.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/APSjfb.JPG/150px-APSjfb.JPG" width="150" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/APSjfb.JPG/225px-APSjfb.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/APSjfb.JPG/300px-APSjfb.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1080" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:APSjfb.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Thomas More at the <a href="/wiki/Ateneo_Law_School" title="Ateneo Law School">Ateneo Law School</a> chapel</div></div></div>
<p>The steadfastness and courage with which More maintained his religious convictions, and his dignity during his imprisonment, trial, and execution, contributed much to More's posthumous reputation, particularly among Roman Catholics. His friend <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a> defended More's character as "more pure than any snow" and described his genius as "such as England never had and never again will have."<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Upon learning of More's execution, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Charles_V" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Charles V">Emperor Charles V</a> said: "Had we been master of such a servant, we would rather have lost the best city of our dominions than such a worthy councillor."<sup id="cite_ref-britannica_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a>, a Roman Catholic convert from the Church of England, predicted More "may come to be counted the greatest Englishman, or at least the greatest historical character in English history."<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Trevor-Roper" title="Hugh Trevor-Roper">Hugh Trevor-Roper</a> called More "the first great Englishman whom we feel that we know, the most saintly of humanists, the most human of saints, the universal man of our cool northern renaissance."<sup id="cite_ref-oconnell_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oconnell-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift">Jonathan Swift</a>, an Anglican, wrote that More was "a person of the greatest virtue this kingdom ever produced".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Some consider Samuel Johnson that quote's author, although neither his writings nor Boswell's contain such.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-chambers_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chambers-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Metaphysical_poet" class="mw-redirect" title="Metaphysical poet">metaphysical poet</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Donne" title="John Donne">John Donne</a>, also honoured as a saint by Anglicans, was More's great-great-nephew.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While Roman Catholic scholars maintain that More used irony in <i>Utopia,</i> and that he remained an orthodox Christian, Marxist theoretician <a href="/wiki/Karl_Kautsky" title="Karl Kautsky">Karl Kautsky</a> considered the book a shrewd critique of economic and social exploitation in pre-modern Europe; More thus influenced the early development of socialist ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Communism.2C_socialism.2C_and_resistance_to_communism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Communism,_socialism,_and_resistance_to_communism">Communism, socialism, and resistance to communism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Communism, socialism, and resistance to communism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span id="SovietCommunism01a"></span>Having been praised "as a <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">Communist</a> hero by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Kautsky" title="Karl Kautsky">Karl Kautsky</a>" because of the Communist attitude to property in his <i>Utopia</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Margaret-L-King01a_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margaret-L-King01a-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> under <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Communism" class="mw-redirect" title="Soviet Communism">Soviet Communism</a> the name of Thomas More was in ninth position from the top<sup id="cite_ref-University-of-Dallas01a_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-University-of-Dallas01a-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>'s Stele of Freedom (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Garden_Obelisk" title="Alexander Garden Obelisk">Obelisk of Revolutionary Thinkers</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-afoniya01a_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afoniya01a-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> as one of the most influential thinkers "who promoted the liberation of humankind from oppression, arbitrariness, and exploitation."<sup id="cite_ref-University-of-Dallas01a_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-University-of-Dallas01a-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> This monument was erected in 1918 in Aleksandrovsky Garden near the <a href="/wiki/Kremlin" class="mw-redirect" title="Kremlin">Kremlin</a> at <a href="/wiki/Lenin" class="mw-redirect" title="Lenin">Lenin</a>'s suggestion.<sup id="cite_ref-Margaret-L-King01a_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margaret-L-King01a-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-University-of-Dallas01a_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-University-of-Dallas01a-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-afoniya01a_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afoniya01a-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It was dismantled on 2 July 2013, during <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a>'s third term as President of <a href="/wiki/Russia#Russian_Federation" title="Russia">post-Communist Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-afoniya01a_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afoniya01a-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Utopia</i> also inspired <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">Socialists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">William Morris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CathEnc02a_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CathEnc02a-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many see More's communism or socialism as purely satirical.<sup id="cite_ref-CathEnc02a_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CathEnc02a-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In 1888, while praising More's communism, Karl Kautsky pointed out that "perplexed" historians and economists often saw the name <i>Utopia</i> (which means "no place") as "a subtle hint by More that he himself regarded his communism as an impracticable dream".<sup id="cite_ref-Kautsky01a_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kautsky01a-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Solzhenitsyn" title="Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn">Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn</a>, the Russian <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize</a>-winning anti-Communist author, and survivor and <a href="/wiki/The_Gulag_Archipelago" title="The Gulag Archipelago">historian of the Soviet prison camps</a>, argued that Soviet communism needed enslavement and forced labour to survive, and that this had been " ...foreseen as far back as Thomas More, the great-grandfather of <a href="/wiki/Socialism_(Marxism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialism (Marxism)">socialism</a>, in his <i>Utopia</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-Bloom01a_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloom01a-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span id="HK-antiCommunist01a"></span>In 2008, More was portrayed on stage in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Allegorical" class="mw-redirect" title="Allegorical">allegorical</a> symbol of the <a href="/wiki/Pro-democracy_camp_in_Hong_Kong" class="mw-redirect" title="Pro-democracy camp in Hong Kong">pan-democracy camp</a> resisting <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communism" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Communism">Chinese Communism</a> in a translated and modified version of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bolt" title="Robert Bolt">Robert Bolt</a>'s play <a href="/wiki/A_Man_for_All_Seasons#HK-antiCommunist01a" title="A Man for All Seasons"><i>A Man for All Seasons</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ChapmanChen01a_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChapmanChen01a-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_popular_culture">Literature and popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Literature and popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/William_Roper_(biographer)" class="mw-redirect" title="William Roper (biographer)">William Roper</a>'s biography of More was one of the first biographies in Modern English.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_More_(play)" title="Sir Thomas More (play)">Sir Thomas More</a></i> is a play written circa 1592 in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Chettle" title="Henry Chettle">Henry Chettle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Munday" title="Anthony Munday">Anthony Munday</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>, and others. In it More is portrayed as a wise and honest statesman. The original manuscript has survived as a handwritten text that shows many revisions by its several authors, as well as the censorious influence of Edmund Tylney, <a href="/wiki/Master_of_the_Revels" title="Master of the Revels">Master of the Revels</a> in the government of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Queen Elizabeth I</a>. The script has since been published and has had several productions.<sup id="cite_ref-Long,_William_B._1989_pages_49_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Long,_William_B._1989_pages_49-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Long,_William_B._1989_pages_49_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Long,_William_B._1989_pages_49-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 20th-century agnostic playwright <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bolt" title="Robert Bolt">Robert Bolt</a> portrayed Thomas More as the <a href="/wiki/Tragic_hero" title="Tragic hero">tragic hero</a> of his 1960 play <i><a href="/wiki/A_Man_for_All_Seasons" title="A Man for All Seasons">A Man for All Seasons</a></i>. The title is drawn from what <a href="/wiki/Robert_Whittington" title="Robert Whittington">Robert Whittington</a> in 1520 wrote of More:
</p>
<blockquote><p>More is a man of an angel's wit and singular learning. I know not his fellow. For where is the man of that gentleness, lowliness and affability? And, as time requireth, a man of marvelous mirth and pastimes, and sometime of as sad gravity. A man for all seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-oconnell_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oconnell-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In 1966, the play, <i>A Man for All Seasons</i>, was adapted into a <a href="/wiki/A_Man_for_All_Seasons_(1966_film)" title="A Man for All Seasons (1966 film)">film</a> with the same title. It was directed by <a href="/wiki/Fred_Zinnemann" title="Fred Zinnemann">Fred Zinnemann</a> and adapted for the screen by the playwright. It stars <a href="/wiki/Paul_Scofield" title="Paul Scofield">Paul Scofield</a>, a noted British actor, who said that the part of Sir Thomas More was "the most difficult part I played."<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The film won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Picture" title="Academy Award for Best Picture">Academy Award for Best Picture</a> and Scofield won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Actor" title="Academy Award for Best Actor">Best Actor Oscar</a>. In 1988 <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a> starred in and directed a made-for-television film that restored the character of "the common man" that had been cut from the 1966 film.
</p><p>Catholic science fiction writer <a href="/wiki/R._A._Lafferty" title="R. A. Lafferty">R. A. Lafferty</a> wrote his novel <i><a href="/wiki/Past_Master_(novel)" title="Past Master (novel)">Past Master</a></i> as a modern equivalent to More's <i>Utopia</i>, which he saw as a satire. In this novel, Thomas More travels through time to the year 2535, where he is made king of the world "Astrobe", only to be beheaded after ruling for a mere nine days. One character compares More favourably to almost every other major historical figure: "He had one completely honest moment right at the end. I cannot think of anyone else who ever had one."
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Karl_Zuchardt" title="Karl Zuchardt">Karl Zuchardt</a>'s novel, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stirb_du_Narr!&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stirb du Narr! (page does not exist)">Stirb du Narr!</a></i> ("Die you fool!"), about More's struggle with <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">King Henry</a>, portrays More as an idealist bound to fail in the power struggle with a ruthless ruler and an unjust world.
</p><p>The novelist <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Mantel" title="Hilary Mantel">Hilary Mantel</a> portrays More as an unsympathetic persecutor of Protestants, and an ally of the Habsburg empire, in her 2009 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Wolf_Hall" title="Wolf Hall">Wolf Hall</a></i>, told from the perspective of a sympathetically portrayed <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell" title="Thomas Cromwell">Thomas Cromwell</a>.
</p><p>Literary critic James Wood in his book <i>The Broken Estate</i>, a collection of essays, is critical of More and refers to him as "cruel in punishment, evasive in argument, lusty for power, and repressive in politics".<sup id="cite_ref-Wood2010_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood2010-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aaron_Zelman" title="Aaron Zelman">Aaron Zelman</a>'s non-fiction book <i>The State Versus the People</i> includes a comparison of <i>Utopia</i> with Plato's <i>Republic</i>. Zelman is undecided as to whether More was being ironic in his book or was genuinely advocating a <a href="/wiki/Police_state" title="Police state">police state</a>. Zelman comments, "More is the only Christian saint to be honoured with a statue at the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Kremlin</a>."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> By this Zelman implies that <i>Utopia</i> influenced <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolsheviks</a>, despite their brutal repression of religion.
</p><p>Other biographers, such as <a href="/wiki/Peter_Ackroyd" title="Peter Ackroyd">Peter Ackroyd</a>, have offered a more sympathetic picture of More as both a sophisticated philosopher and man of letters, as well as a zealous Catholic who believed in the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a> over <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>.
</p><p>The protagonist of <a href="/wiki/Walker_Percy" title="Walker Percy">Walker Percy</a>'s novels, <i><a href="/wiki/Love_in_the_Ruins" title="Love in the Ruins">Love in the Ruins</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Thanatos_Syndrome" title="The Thanatos Syndrome">The Thanatos Syndrome</a></i>, is "Dr Thomas More", a reluctant Catholic and descendant of More.
</p><p>More is the focus of the <a href="/wiki/Al_Stewart" title="Al Stewart">Al Stewart</a> song "A Man For All Seasons" from the 1978 album <i><a href="/wiki/Time_Passages" title="Time Passages">Time Passages</a></i>, and of the <a href="/wiki/Far_(band)" title="Far (band)">Far</a> song "Sir", featured on the limited editions and 2008 re-release of their 1994 album <i><a href="/wiki/Quick_(album)" class="mw-redirect" title="Quick (album)">Quick</a></i>. In addition, the song "<a href="/wiki/So_Says_I" title="So Says I">So Says I</a>" by indie rock outfit <a href="/wiki/The_Shins" title="The Shins">The Shins</a> alludes to the socialist interpretation of More's <i>Utopia</i>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Northam" title="Jeremy Northam">Jeremy Northam</a> depicts More in the television series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tudors" title="The Tudors">The Tudors</a> </i>as a peaceful man, as well as a devout Roman Catholic and loving family patriarch. He also shows More loathing Protestantism, burning both Martin Luther's books and English Protestants who have been convicted of heresy. The portrayal has unhistorical aspects, such as that More neither personally caused nor attended <a href="/wiki/Simon_Fish" title="Simon Fish">Simon Fish</a>'s execution (since Fish actually died of <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> in 1531 before he could stand trial), although More's <i>The Supplycatyon of Soulys</i>, published in October 1529, addressed Fish's <a href="/wiki/Simon_Fish#Supplycatyon_of_Soulys:_St._Thomas_More&#39;s_Response_to_Simon_Fish" title="Simon Fish"><i>Supplication for the Beggars</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FishOnline1_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FishOnline1-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MoreVsFish1_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MoreVsFish1-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, there is no evidence that More ever attended the execution of any heretic. The series also neglected to show More's avowed insistence that Richard Rich's testimony about More disputing the King's title as <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Head_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Head of the Church of England">Supreme Head of the Church of England</a> was perjured.
</p><p>In 2002, More was placed at number 37 in the BBC's poll of the <a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons" title="100 Greatest Britons">100 Greatest Britons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutions_named_after_More">Institutions named after More</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Institutions named after More">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_institutions_named_after_Thomas_More" title="List of institutions named after Thomas More">List of institutions named after Thomas More</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_sites">Historic sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Historic sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(February 2018)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Westminster_Hall">Westminster Hall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Westminster Hall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A plaque in the middle of the floor of London's <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Hall">Westminster Hall</a> commemorates More's trial for treason and condemnation to execution in that original part of the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Palace of Westminster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tms_hall_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tms_hall-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The building, which houses Parliament, would have been well known to More, who served several terms as a member and became Speaker of the House of Commons before his appointment as England's Lord Chancellor.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crosby_Hall">Crosby Hall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Crosby Hall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Crown confiscated More's home and estate along the Thames in Chelsea after his execution. Crosby Hall, which was part of More's London residence, was eventually relocated and reconstructed in Chelsea by conservation architect <a href="/wiki/Walter_Godfrey" title="Walter Godfrey">Walter Godfrey</a> in 1910. Rebuilt in the 1990s, the white stone building stands amid modern brick structures that attempt to recapture the style of More's former manor on the site. Crosby Hall is privately owned and closed to the public. The modern structures face the Thames and include an entry way that displays More's arms, heraldic beasts, and a Latin maxim. Apartment buildings and a park cover the former gardens and orchard; Roper's Garden is the park atop one of More's gardens, sunken as his was believed to be. No other remnants exist of the More estate.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chelsea_Old_Church">Chelsea Old Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Chelsea Old Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chelsea_Old_Church_14.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chelsea_Old_Church_14.JPG/220px-Chelsea_Old_Church_14.JPG" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chelsea_Old_Church_14.JPG/330px-Chelsea_Old_Church_14.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chelsea_Old_Church_14.JPG/440px-Chelsea_Old_Church_14.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1944" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chelsea_Old_Church_14.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thomas More statue, Chelsea Old Church</div></div></div>
<p>Across a small park and Old Church Street from Crosby Hall is <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Old_Church" title="Chelsea Old Church">Chelsea Old Church</a>, an Anglican church whose southern chapel More commissioned and in which he sang with the parish choir. Except for his chapel, the church was largely destroyed in the Second World War and rebuilt in 1958. The capitals on the medieval arch connecting the chapel to the main sanctuary display symbols associated with More and his office. On the southern wall of the sanctuary is the tomb and epitaph he erected for himself and his wives, detailing his ancestry and accomplishments in Latin, including his role as peacemaker between the Christian nations of Europe as well as a curiously altered portion about his curbing heresy. When More served Mass, he would leave by the door just to the left of it. He is not, however, buried here, nor is it entirely certain which of his family may be. It is open to the public at specific times. Outside the church, facing the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a>, is a statue by L. Cubitt Bevis erected in 1969, commemorating More as "saint", "scholar", and "statesman"; the back displays his coat-of-arms. Nearby, on Upper Cheyne Row, the Roman Catholic Church of Our Most Holy Redeemer &amp; St. Thomas More honours the martyr.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tower_Hill">Tower Hill</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Tower Hill">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A plaque and small garden commemorate the famed execution site on Tower Hill, London, just outside the Tower of London, as well as all those executed there, many as religious martyrs or as prisoners of conscience. More's corpse, minus his head, was unceremoniously buried in an unmarked mass grave beneath the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St_Peter_ad_Vincula" title="Church of St Peter ad Vincula">Royal Chapel of St. Peter Ad Vincula</a>, within the walls of the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>, as was the custom for traitors executed at Tower Hill. The chapel is accessible to Tower visitors.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="St_Katharine_Docks">St Katharine Docks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: St Katharine Docks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thomas More is commemorated by a stone <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_plaque" title="Commemorative plaque">plaque</a> near <a href="/wiki/St_Katharine_Docks" title="St Katharine Docks">St Katharine Docks</a>, just east of the Tower where he was executed. The street in which it is situated was formerly called Nightingale Lane, a corruption of "Knighten Guild", derived from the original owners of the land. It is now renamed Thomas More Street in his honour.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="St_Dunstan.27s_Church_and_Roper_House.2C_Canterbury"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="St_Dunstan's_Church_and_Roper_House,_Canterbury">St Dunstan's Church and Roper House, Canterbury</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: St Dunstan&#039;s Church and Roper House, Canterbury">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/St._Dunstan%27s,_Canterbury" title="St. Dunstan&#39;s, Canterbury">St Dunstan's Church</a>, an Anglican parish church in Canterbury, possesses More's head, rescued by his daughter <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Roper" title="Margaret Roper">Margaret Roper</a>, whose family lived in Canterbury down and across the street from their parish church. A stone immediately to the left of the altar marks the sealed Roper family vault beneath the Nicholas Chapel, itself to the right of the church's sanctuary or main altar. <a href="/wiki/St._Dunstan%27s,_Canterbury" title="St. Dunstan&#39;s, Canterbury">St Dunstan's Church</a> has carefully investigated, preserved and sealed this burial vault. The last archaeological investigation revealed that the suspected head of More rests in a niche separate from the other bodies, possibly from later interference.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Displays in the chapel record the archaeological findings in pictures and narratives. Roman Catholics donated stained glass to commemorate the events in More's life. A small plaque marks the former home of William and Margaret Roper; another house nearby and entitled Roper House is now a home for the deaf.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Note: The reference "CW" is to the relevant volume of the <i><a href="/wiki/Yale_Edition_of_the_Complete_Works_of_St._Thomas_More" title="Yale Edition of the Complete Works of St. Thomas More">Yale Edition of the Complete Works of St. Thomas More</a></i> (New Haven and London 1963–1997)
</p>
<h3><span id="Published_during_More.27s_life_.28with_dates_of_publication.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Published_during_More's_life_(with_dates_of_publication)">Published during More's life (with dates of publication)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Published during More&#039;s life (with dates of publication)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/Utopia_(book)" title="Utopia (book)">Utopia</a></i> (1516) (CW 4)</li>
<li><i>Latin Poems</i> (1518, 1520) (CW 3, Pt.2)</li>
<li><i>Letter to Brixius</i> (1520) (CW 3, Pt. 2, App C)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Responsio_ad_Lutherum" title="Responsio ad Lutherum">Responsio ad Lutherum</a></i> (The Answer to Luther, 1523) (CW 5)</li>
<li><i>A Dialogue Concerning Heresies</i> (1529, 1530) (CW 6)</li>
<li><i>Supplication of Souls</i> (1529) (CW 7)</li>
<li><i>Letter Against Frith</i> (1532) (CW 7)</li>
<li><i>The Confutation of Tyndale's Answer</i> (1532, 1533) (CW 8)</li>
<li><i>Apology</i> (1533) (CW 9)</li>
<li><i>Debellation of Salem and Bizance</i> (1533) (CW 10)</li>
<li><i>The Answer to a Poisoned Book</i> (1533) (CW 11)</li></ul>
<h3><span id="Published_after_More.27s_death_.28with_likely_dates_of_composition.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Published_after_More's_death_(with_likely_dates_of_composition)">Published after More's death (with likely dates of composition)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Published after More&#039;s death (with likely dates of composition)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li><i>Treatise Upon the Passion</i> (1534) (CW 13)</li>
<li><i>Treatise on the Blessed Body</i> (1535) (CW 13)</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Translations">Translations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_More&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Translations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-afoniya01a-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-afoniya01a_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-afoniya01a_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-afoniya01a_8-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-afoniya01a_8-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">afoniya (10 July 2013). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://afoniya.wordpress.com/2013/07/10/on-the-removal-of-a-moscow-statue/">"On the removal of a Moscow statue"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">20 December</span> 2014</span>. <q>What was known as the Stele of Freedom or the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Garden_Obelisk" title="Alexander Garden Obelisk">Obelisk of Revolutionary Thinkers</a> has been dismantled apparently to be reinstalled in some months time as a monument to the Romanov Dynasty. This historically symbolic act was carried out on July 2 completely unannounced …  The obelisk was one of the most interesting statues historically and ideologically because of the kind of names that it had on the statue. This was not simply a case of Marx, Engels, Lenin. It was (it seems) the first revolutionary monument to be opened after the revolution of 1917 and, in a non-dogmatic spirit, it included the names of anarchists, reformist socialists and even that of Thomas More.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=On+the+removal+of+a+Moscow+statue&amp;rft.date=2013-07-10&amp;rft.au=afoniya&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fafoniya.wordpress.com%2F2013%2F07%2F10%2Fon-the-removal-of-a-moscow-statue%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThomas+More" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-Wegemer1996a-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Wegemer1996a_38-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Gerard_Wegemer" title="Gerard Wegemer">Wegemer, Gerard</a> (1996). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.ie/books?id=RazaAAAAMAAJ&amp;q=%22thomas+more%22+german+peasants+Revolt&amp;dq=%22thomas+more%22+german+peasants+Revolt&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0ahUKEwj2n43VxdrdAhVGJsAKHQp6DoEQ6AEIVTAI"><i>Thomas More on statesmanship</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/Catholic_University_of_America_Press" title="Catholic University of America Press">Catholic University of America Press </a>. p.&#160;173. <q>...civil chaos will surely follow (691-93). This prediction seemed to come true very quickly, as More noted in his next polemical work, A dialogue Concerning Heresies. There he argued that the Peasants' Revolt in Germany (1525), the Lutheran mercenaries' sack of Rome (1527), and the growing unrest in England all stemmed from Luther's inflammatory teachings and especially the lure of false freedom</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Thomas+More+on+statesmanship&amp;rft.pages=173&amp;rft.pub=Catholic+University+of+America+Press&amp;rft.date=1996&amp;rft.aulast=Wegemer&amp;rft.aufirst=Gerard&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.ie%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DRazaAAAAMAAJ%26q%3D%2522thomas%2Bmore%2522%2Bgerman%2Bpeasants%2BRevolt%26dq%3D%2522thomas%2Bmore%2522%2Bgerman%2Bpeasants%2BRevolt%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ved%3D0ahUKEwj2n43VxdrdAhVGJsAKHQp6DoEQ6AEIVTAI&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThomas+More" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/> Original from	the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> Digitized	29 Jul 2009</span>

<li id="cite_note-Ackroyd-ch22a-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Ackroyd-ch22a_39-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Peter_Ackroyd" title="Peter Ackroyd">Peter Ackroyd</a> (2012). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=8J9uNOydymUC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=ackroyd+thomas+more&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0ahUKEwitncealo7VAhUM9IMKHZu6A3kQ6AEIKDAA#v=onepage&amp;q=burned%20as%20there%20was%20neuer%20wretche%20I%20wene%20better%20worthy&amp;f=false"><i>The Life of Thomas More</i></a>. Knopf Doubleday Publishing Group. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0307823016" title="Special:BookSources/0307823016">0307823016</a>. <q>(Chapter 22) ... Already, in these early days of English heresy, he was thinking of the fire. It is a measure of his alarm at the erosion of the traditional order that he should, in this letter, compose a defence of scholastic theology—the same scholasticism which in his younger days he had treated with derision. This was no longer a time for questioning, or innovation, or uncertainty, of any kind. He blamed Luther for the Peasants’ Revolt in Germany, and maintained that all its havoc and destruction were the direct result of Luther’s challenge to the authority of the Church; under the pretext of ‘libertas’ Luther preached ‘licentia’ which had in turn led to rape, sacrilege, bloodshed, fire and ruin.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Life+of+Thomas+More&amp;rft.pub=Knopf+Doubleday+Publishing+Group&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.isbn=0307823016&amp;rft.au=Peter+Ackroyd&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D8J9uNOydymUC%26printsec%3Dfrontcover%26dq%3Dackroyd%2Bthomas%2Bmore%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ved%3D0ahUKEwitncealo7VAhUM9IMKHZu6A3kQ6AEIKDAA%23v%3Donepage%26q%3Dburned%2520as%2520there%2520was%2520neuer%2520wretche%2520I%2520wene%2520better%2520worthy%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThomas+More" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. </li>
<li id="cite_note-JoannePaul2016a-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-JoannePaul2016a_40-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Joanne Paul (2016). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.ie/books?id=KN2qDQAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PT140&amp;dq=%22thomas+more%22+german+peasants+Revolt&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0ahUKEwj2n43VxdrdAhVGJsAKHQp6DoEQ6AEILzAB#v=onepage&amp;q=%22thomas%20more%22%20german%20peasants%20Revolt&amp;f=false"><i>Thomas More</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Wiley_%26_Sons" title="John Wiley &amp; Sons">John Wiley &amp; Sons</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780745692203" title="Special:BookSources/9780745692203">9780745692203</a>. <q>Princes were 'driven by necessity' by the 'importune malice of heretics raising rebellions' to set 'sorer and sorer punishments thereunto' (CTA, 956). In other words, the heretics had started it: 'the Catholic Church did never persecute heretics by any temporal pain or any secular power until the heretics began such violence themself' (CTA, 954). More had in mind violent conflicts on the continent, such as the German Peasants' War (1524–5) and the Münster Rebellion (1532–5).</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Thomas+More&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft.isbn=9780745692203&amp;rft.au=Joanne+Paul&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.ie%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DKN2qDQAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPT140%26dq%3D%2522thomas%2Bmore%2522%2Bgerman%2Bpeasants%2BRevolt%26hl%3Den%26sa%3DX%26ved%3D0ahUKEwj2n43VxdrdAhVGJsAKHQp6DoEQ6AEILzAB%23v%3Donepage%26q%3D%2522thomas%2520more%2522%2520german%2520peasants%2520Revolt%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThomas+More" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears.
